</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Hindu_scriptures_and_texts" title="Template:Hindu scriptures and texts"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Hindu_scriptures_and_texts" title="Template talk:Hindu scriptures and texts"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Hindu_scriptures_and_texts&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atharva-Veda_samhita_page_471_illustration.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3106" data-file-width="2313" decoding="async" height="349" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Atharva-Veda_samhita_page_471_illustration.png/260px-Atharva-Veda_samhita_page_471_illustration.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Atharva-Veda_samhita_page_471_illustration.png/390px-Atharva-Veda_samhita_page_471_illustration.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Atharva-Veda_samhita_page_471_illustration.png/520px-Atharva-Veda_samhita_page_471_illustration.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atharva-Veda_samhita_page_471_illustration.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Vedas are ancient Sanskrit texts of Hinduism. Above: A page from the <i><a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Vedas</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/eÉª/: 'a' in 'face'">eÉª</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAST" title="IAST">IAST</a>: <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">veda</i></span></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">à¤µà¥à¤¦à¤</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a>â</small>'knowledge') are a large body of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_texts" title="Religious texts">religious texts</a> originating in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_India" title="Ancient India">ancient India</a>. Composed in <a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">Vedic Sanskrit</a>, the texts constitute the oldest layer of <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_literature" title="Sanskrit literature">Sanskrit literature</a> and the oldest <a href="/wiki/Hindu_texts" title="Hindu texts">scriptures</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are four Vedas: the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajurveda</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Samaveda" title="Samaveda">Samaveda</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gflood_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-gflood-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> Each Veda has four subdivisions â the <a href="/wiki/Samhita" title="Samhita">Samhitas</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benediction" title="Benediction">benedictions</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Aranyaka" title="Aranyaka">Aranyakas</a> (text on rituals, ceremonies, sacrifices and symbolic-sacrifices), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmanas" title="Brahmanas">Brahmanas</a> (commentaries on rituals, ceremonies and sacrifices), and the <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> (texts discussing <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a>, philosophy and spiritual knowledge).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gflood_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-gflood-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Bhattacharya_2006_pp._8â14_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Bhattacharya_2006_pp._8â14-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jan_Gonda_1975_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jan_Gonda_1975-12">[11]</a></sup> Some scholars add a fifth category â the <a href="/wiki/Upasana" title="Upasana">Upasanas</a> (worship).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya20068â14_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya20068â14-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1995351â357_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1995351â357-14">[13]</a></sup> The texts of the Upanishads discuss ideas akin to the heterodox <i>sramana</i>-traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199682_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199682-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vedas are <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aruti" title="Åruti">Åruti</a></i></span></i> ("what is heard"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEApte1965887_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEApte1965887-16">[15]</a></sup> distinguishing them from other religious texts, which are called <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sm%E1%B9%9Bti" title="Smá¹ti">smá¹ti</a></i></span></i> ("what is remembered"). Hindus consider the Vedas to be <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apauru%E1%B9%A3eya" title="Apauruá¹£eya">apauruá¹£eya</a></i>, which means "not of a man, superhuman"<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEApte1965"apauruSeya"_17-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEApte1965"apauruSeya"-17'>[16]</a></sup> and "impersonal, authorless,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma2011196â197_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma2011196â197-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWesterhoff2009290_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWesterhoff2009290-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd2013128_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd2013128-20">[19]</a></sup> revelations of sacred sounds and texts heard by ancient <a href="/wiki/Sage_(philosophy)" title="Sage (philosophy)">sages</a> after intense meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPollock201141â58_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPollock201141â58-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScharfe200213â14_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScharfe200213â14-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Vedas have been orally transmitted since the 2nd millennium BCE with the help of elaborate <a href="/wiki/Vedic_chant" title="Vedic chant">mnemonic techniques</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood2007_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2007-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHexam2011chapter_8_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHexam2011chapter_8-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013-25">[24]</a></sup> The mantras, the oldest part of the Vedas, are recited in the modern age for their phonology rather than the semantics, and are considered to be "primordial rhythms of creation", preceding the forms to which they refer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996347_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996347-26">[25]</a></sup> By reciting them the cosmos is regenerated, "by enlivening and nourishing the forms of creation at their base."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996347_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996347-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The various <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian philosophies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindu_denominations" title="Hindu denominations">Hindu denominations</a> have taken differing positions on the Vedas; schools of Indian philosophy which acknowledge the primal authority of the Vedas are classified as "orthodox" (<a href="/wiki/%C4%80stika_and_n%C4%81stika" title="Ästika and nÄstika">Ästika</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freschi_2012_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freschi_2012-27">[note 2]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Arama%E1%B9%87a" title="Åramaá¹a">Åramaá¹a</a> traditions, such as <a href="/wiki/Charvaka" title="Charvaka">Charvaka</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ajivika" title="Ajivika">Ajivika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, which did not regard the Vedas as authorities, are referred to as "heterodox" or "non-orthodox" (<a href="/wiki/%C4%80stika_and_n%C4%81stika" title="Ästika and nÄstika">nÄstika</a>) schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199682_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199682-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_astika_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB_astika-28">[26]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_usage">Etymology and usage</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">vÃ©da</i></span></i> "knowledge, wisdom"  is derived from the root <i>vid-</i> "to know". This is reconstructed as being derived from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> root <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">*uÌ¯eid-</span></span></i>, meaning "see" or "know."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonier-Williams1899[httpwwwibiblioorgsripediaebooksmw1000mw_1048html_p._1015]_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonier-Williams1899[httpwwwibiblioorgsripediaebooksmw1000mw_1048html_p._1015]-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEApte1965856_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEApte1965856-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The noun is from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">*uÌ¯eidos</span></span></i>, cognate to <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">(Ï)Îµá¼¶Î´Î¿Ï</span></span> "aspect", "form" . This is not to be confused with the homonymous 1st and 3rd person singular perfect tense <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">vÃ©da</i></span></i>, cognate to Greek <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">(Ï)Î¿á¼¶Î´Î±</span></span> <i>(w)oida</i> "I know". Root cognates are Greek <a href="/wiki/Idea" title="Idea">á¼°Î´Î­Î±</a>, English <i><a href="/wiki/Wit" title="Wit">wit</a></i>, etc., <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>videÅ</i> "I see", German <i>wissen</i> "to know" etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sanskrit term <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">veda</i></span></i> as a common noun means "knowledge".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonier-Williams1899[httpwwwibiblioorgsripediaebooksmw1000mw_1048html_p._1015]_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonier-Williams1899[httpwwwibiblioorgsripediaebooksmw1000mw_1048html_p._1015]-29">[27]</a></sup> The term in some contexts, such as hymn 10.93.11 of the <i><a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a></i>, means "obtaining or finding wealth, property",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonier-Williams1899[httpwwwibiblioorgsripediaebooksmw1000mw_1050html_p._1017_(2nd_Column)]_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonier-Williams1899[httpwwwibiblioorgsripediaebooksmw1000mw_1050html_p._1017_(2nd_Column)]-32">[30]</a></sup> while in some others it means "a bunch of grass together" as in a broom or for <a href="/wiki/Homa_(ritual)" title="Homa (ritual)">ritual fire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonier-Williams1899[httpwwwibiblioorgsripediaebooksmw1000mw_1050html_p._1017_(3rd_Column)]_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonier-Williams1899[httpwwwibiblioorgsripediaebooksmw1000mw_1050html_p._1017_(3rd_Column)]-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vedas are called <i>Maá¹ai</i> or <i>Vaymoli</i> in parts of South India. Marai literally means "hidden, a secret, mystery". But the Tamil <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Naan_Marai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Naan Marai (page does not exist)">Naan Marai</a> mentioned in <a href="/wiki/Tolk%C4%81ppiyam" title="TolkÄppiyam">Tholkappiam</a> is not Sanskrit Vedas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Carman_1989_pp._259â261_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Carman_1989_pp._259â261-35">[33]</a></sup> In some parts of <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a> (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Iyengar" title="Iyengar">Iyengar</a> communities), the word <i>veda</i> is used in the <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a> writings of the <a href="/wiki/Alvars" title="Alvars">Alvar</a> saints. Such writings include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divya_Prabandham" title="Divya Prabandham">Divya Prabandham</a> (aka <a href="/wiki/Tiruvaymoli" title="Tiruvaymoli">Tiruvaymoli</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasudha_Narayanan_1994_pp._43,_117_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vasudha_Narayanan_1994_pp._43,_117-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vedic_texts">Vedic texts</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rigveda_MS2097.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1056" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Rigveda_MS2097.jpg/260px-Rigveda_MS2097.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Rigveda_MS2097.jpg/390px-Rigveda_MS2097.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Rigveda_MS2097.jpg/520px-Rigveda_MS2097.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rigveda_MS2097.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Rigveda</i> manuscript in <a href="/wiki/Devanagari" title="Devanagari">Devanagari</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vedic_Sanskrit_corpus">Vedic Sanskrit corpus</span></h3>
<p>The term "Vedic texts" is used in two distinct meanings:
</p>
<ol><li>Texts composed in <a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">Vedic Sanskrit</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic period</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_Age_India" title="Iron Age India">Iron Age India</a>)</li>
<li>Any text considered as "connected to the Vedas" or a "corollary of the Vedas"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>The corpus of <a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">Vedic Sanskrit</a> texts includes:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samhitas" title="Samhitas">Samhitas</a> (Sanskrit <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">saá¹hitÄ</i></span></i>, "collection"), are collections of metric texts ("<a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantras</a>"). There are four "Vedic" Samhitas: the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rig-Veda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajur-Veda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samaveda" title="Samaveda">Sama-Veda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharva-Veda</a>, most of which are available in several <a href="/wiki/Recension" title="Recension">recensions</a> (<i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">ÅÄkhÄ</i></span></i>). In some contexts, the term <i>Veda</i> is used to refer only to these Samhitas, the collection of mantras. This is the oldest layer of Vedic texts, which were composed between circa 1500â1200 BCE (Rig Veda book 2â9),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dating_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-dating-2">[note 1]</a></sup> and 1200â900 BCE for the other <i>Samhitas</i>. The Samhitas contain invocations to deities like <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agni" title="Agni">Agni</a>, "to secure their benediction for success in battles or for welfare of the clan."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrasad2020150_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrasad2020150-38">[36]</a></sup> The complete corpus of Vedic mantras as collected in <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Bloomfield" title="Maurice Bloomfield">Bloomfield</a>'s <i>Vedic Concordance</i> (1907) consists of some 89,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pada_(Hindu_mythology)" title="Pada (Hindu mythology)">padas</a> (<a href="/wiki/Foot_(prosody)" title="Foot (prosody)">metrical feet</a>), of which 72,000 occur in the four Samhitas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Brahmana" title="Brahmana">Brahmanas</a> are prose texts that comment and explain the solemn rituals as well as expound on their meaning and many connected themes. Each of the Brahmanas is associated with one of the Samhitas or its recensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier199467â69_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier199467â69-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebri_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebri-41">[39]</a></sup> The oldest dated to about 900 BCE, while the youngest Brahmanas (such as the <a href="/wiki/Shatapatha_Brahmana" title="Shatapatha Brahmana">Shatapatha Brahmana</a>), were complete by about 700 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcp_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-bcp-43">[41]</a></sup> The Brahmanas may either form separate texts or can be partly integrated into the text of the Samhitas. They may also include the Aranyakas and Upanishads.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Aranyaka" title="Aranyaka">Aranyakas</a>, "wilderness texts" or "forest treaties", were composed by people who meditated in the woods as recluses and are the third part of the Vedas. The texts contain discussions and interpretations of ceremonies, from ritualistic to symbolic meta-ritualistic points of view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jago_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-jago-44">[42]</a></sup> It is frequently read in secondary literature.</li>
<li>Older <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mukhya_Upanishads" title="Mukhya Upanishads">Mukhya Upanishads</a> (<a href="/wiki/Brihadaranyaka_Upanishad" title="Brihadaranyaka Upanishad"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Bá¹hadÄraá¹yaka</i></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Chandogya_Upanishad" title="Chandogya Upanishad">Chandogya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katha_Upanishad" title="Katha Upanishad"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Kaá¹­ha</i></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Kena_Upanishad" title="Kena Upanishad">Kena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aitareya_Upanishad" title="Aitareya Upanishad">Aitareya</a>, and others),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michaels_2004_51_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michaels_2004_51-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood_2003_69_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Flood_2003_69-1">[1]</a></sup> composed between 800 BCE and the end of the Vedic period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahony1998p271_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahony1998p271-46">[44]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> are largely philosophical works, some in dialogue form. They are the foundation of Hindu philosophical thought and its diverse traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wendydoniger_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-wendydoniger-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> Of the Vedic corpus, they alone are widely known, and the central ideas of the Upanishads are still influential in Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wendydoniger_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-wendydoniger-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li>The texts considered "Vedic" in the sense of "corollaries of the Vedas" are less clearly defined, and may include numerous post-Vedic texts such as the later <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C5%ABtra" title="SÅ«tra">Sutra literature</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shrauta_Sutras" title="Shrauta Sutras">Shrauta Sutras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kalpa_(Vedanga)#Gryhasutras" title="Kalpa (Vedanga)">Gryha Sutras</a>, which are smriti texts. Together, the Vedas and these Sutras form part of the Vedic Sanskrit corpus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood_2003_69_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Flood_2003_69-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[note 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[note 4]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>While production of Brahmanas and Aranyakas ceased with the end of the Vedic period, additional Upanishads were composed after the end of the Vedic period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel2003100â101_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel2003100â101-52">[48]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Brahmana" title="Brahmana">Brahmanas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aranyaka" title="Aranyaka">Aranyakas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a>, among other things, interpret and discuss the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samhitas" title="Samhitas">Samhitas</a> in philosophical and metaphorical ways to explore abstract concepts such as the Absolute (<a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>), and the soul or the self (<a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ätman (Hinduism)">Atman</a>), introducing <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> philosophy, one of the major trends of later <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>. In other parts, they show evolution of ideas, such as from actual sacrifice to symbolic sacrifice, and of spirituality in the Upanishads. This has inspired later Hindu scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Adi Shankara</a> to classify each Veda into <i>karma-kanda</i> (à¤à¤°à¥à¤® à¤à¤£à¥à¤¡, action/sacrificial ritual-related sections, the <i>Samhitas</i> and <i>Brahmanas</i>); and <i>jnana-kanda</i> (à¤à¥à¤à¤¾à¤¨ à¤à¤£à¥à¤¡, knowledge/spirituality-related sections, mainly the Upanishads').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartley2001490_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartley2001490-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege199630_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege199630-54">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura1983409_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura1983409-55">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya20069_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya20069-56">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnapp200510â11_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnapp200510â11-57">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roer_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roer-58">[note 5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id=".C5.9Aruti_and_smriti"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Åruti_and_smriti">Åruti and smriti</span></h3>
<p>Vedas are <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aruti" title="Åruti">Åruti</a></i></span></i> ("what is heard"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEApte1965887_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEApte1965887-16">[15]</a></sup> distinguishing them from other religious texts, which are called <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sm%E1%B9%9Bti" title="Smá¹ti">smá¹ti</a></i></span></i> ("what is remembered"). This indigenous system of categorization was adopted by <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max MÃ¼ller">Max MÃ¼ller</a> and, while it is subject to some debate, it is still widely used. As Axel Michaels explains:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>These classifications are often not tenable for linguistic and formal reasons: There is not only <i>one</i> collection at any one time, but rather several handed down in separate Vedic schools; UpaniÅads [...] are sometimes not to be distinguished from <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Äraá¹yakas</i></span> [...]; <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">BrÄhmaá¹as</i></span> contain older strata of language attributed to the <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Saá¹hitÄs</i></span>; there are various dialects and locally prominent traditions of the Vedic schools. Nevertheless, it is advisable to stick to the division adopted by Max MÃ¼ller because it follows the Indian tradition, conveys the historical sequence fairly accurately, and underlies the current editions, translations, and monographs on Vedic literature."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michaels_2004_51_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Michaels_2004_51-45">[43]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Among the widely known <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aruti" title="Åruti">Årutis</a> include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veda" title="Veda">Vedas</a> and their embedded textsâthe <a href="/wiki/Samhita" title="Samhita">Samhitas</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upanishad" title="Upanishad">Upanishads</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Brahmana" title="Brahmana">Brahmanas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aranyaka" title="Aranyaka">Aranyakas</a>. The well-known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sm%E1%B9%9Bti" title="Smá¹ti">smá¹tis</a> include <a href="/wiki/Bhagavad_Gita" title="Bhagavad Gita">Bhagavad Gita</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagavata_Purana" title="Bhagavata Purana">Bhagavata Purana</a> and the epics <a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a>, amongst others.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authorship">Authorship</span></h3>
<p>Hindus consider the Vedas to be <i><a href="/wiki/Apauru%E1%B9%A3ey%C4%81" title="Apauruá¹£eyÄ">apauruá¹£eyÄ</a></i>, which means "not of a man, superhuman"<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEApte1965"apauruSeya"_17-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEApte1965"apauruSeya"-17'>[16]</a></sup> and "impersonal, authorless."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma2011196â197_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma2011196â197-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWesterhoff2009290_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWesterhoff2009290-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd2013128_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd2013128-20">[19]</a></sup> The Vedas, for orthodox Indian theologians, are considered revelations seen by ancient <a href="/wiki/Sage_(philosophy)" title="Sage (philosophy)">sages</a> after intense meditation, and texts that have been more carefully preserved since ancient times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPollock201141â58_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPollock201141â58-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScharfe200213â14_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScharfe200213â14-22">[21]</a></sup> In the Hindu Epic <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i>, the creation of Vedas is credited to <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[54]</a></sup> The Vedic hymns themselves assert that they were skillfully created by <i><a href="/wiki/Rishi" title="Rishi">Rishis</a></i> (sages), after inspired creativity, just as a carpenter builds a chariot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScharfe200213â14_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScharfe200213â14-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[note 6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest part of the Rig Veda <i>Samhita</i> was orally composed in north-western India (<a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a>) between <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500 and 1200Â BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dating_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-dating-2">[note 1]</a></sup> while book 10 of the Rig Veda, and the other Samhitas were composed between 1200 and 900 BCE more eastward, between the Yamuna and the Ganges, the heartland of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aryavarta" title="Aryavarta">Aryavarta</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kuru_Kingdom" title="Kuru Kingdom">Kuru Kingdom</a> (c. 1200 â c. 900 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel19954_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel19954-62">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199637_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199637-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony200749_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony200749-63">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel200868_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel200868-64">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrazier2011344_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrazier2011344-65">[59]</a></sup> The "circum-Vedic" texts, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Shakha" title="Shakha">redaction</a> of the Samhitas, date to c. 1000â500 BCE.
</p><p>According to tradition, <i><a href="/wiki/Vyasa" title="Vyasa">Vyasa</a></i> is the compiler of the Vedas, who arranged the four kinds of <i>mantras</i> into four <i>Samhitas</i> (Collections).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege2012249,_250_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege2012249,_250-66">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidUCEoAwAAQBAJpgPT16_16]_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidUCEoAwAAQBAJpgPT16_16]-67">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Chronology.2C_transmission.2C_and_interpretation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology,_transmission,_and_interpretation">Chronology, transmission, and interpretation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic period</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">Chronology</span></h3>
<p>The Vedas are among the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_literature" title="Ancient literature">oldest sacred texts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDutt200636_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDutt200636-68">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGomes201254_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGomes201254-69">[63]</a></sup> The bulk of the Rigveda Samhita was composed in the northwestern region (Punjab) of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, most likely between <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500 and 1200Â BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199637_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199637-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel19954_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel19954-62">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007454_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007454-70">[64]</a></sup> although a wider approximation of <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1700â1100Â BC has also been given.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOberlies1998158_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOberlies1998158-71">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKumar2014179_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKumar2014179-72">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dating_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-dating-2">[note 1]</a></sup> The other three Samhitas are considered to date from the time of the <a href="/wiki/Kuru_Kingdom" title="Kuru Kingdom">Kuru Kingdom</a>, approximately <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1200â900 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood_2003_69_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Flood_2003_69-1">[1]</a></sup> The "circum-Vedic" texts, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Shakha" title="Shakha">redaction</a> of the Samhitas, date to c. 1000â500 BCE, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic period</a>, spanning the mid 2nd to mid 1st millennium BCE, or the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_India" title="Bronze Age India">Late Bronze Age</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_Age_India" title="Iron Age India">Iron Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[note 7]</a></sup>
The Vedic period reaches its peak only after the composition of the mantra texts, with the establishment of the various <a href="/wiki/Shakha" title="Shakha">shakhas</a> all over Northern India which annotated the mantra <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samhitas" title="Samhitas">samhitas</a> with <a href="/wiki/Brahmana" title="Brahmana">Brahmana</a> discussions of their meaning, and reaches its end in the age of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a> and <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="PÄá¹ini">Panini</a> and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Mahajanapadas" title="Mahajanapadas">Mahajanapadas</a> (archaeologically, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Black_Polished_Ware" title="Northern Black Polished Ware">Northern Black Polished Ware</a>). Michael Witzel gives a time span of c. 1500 to c. 500â400 BCE. Witzel makes special reference to the Near Eastern <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_superstrate_in_Mitanni" title="Indo-Aryan superstrate in Mitanni">Mitanni material</a> of the 14th century BCE, the only epigraphic record of Indo-Aryan contemporary to the Rigvedic period. He gives 150 BCE (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pata%C3%B1jali" title="PataÃ±jali">PataÃ±jali</a>) as a <a href="/wiki/Terminus_post_quem" title="Terminus post quem">terminus ante quem</a> for all Vedic Sanskrit literature, and 1200 BCE (the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_Age_India" title="Iron Age India">Iron Age</a>) as <a href="/wiki/Terminus_post_quem" title="Terminus post quem">terminus post quem</a> for the Atharvaveda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel200368_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel200368-74">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h3>
<p>The Vedas were orally transmitted since their composition in the <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic period</a> for several millennia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witzel2003_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witzel2003-75">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood2007_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2007-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1995344_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1995344-76">[69]</a></sup> The authoritative transmission<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996345_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996345-77">[70]</a></sup> of the Vedas is by an <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a> in a <i><a href="/wiki/Sampradaya" title="Sampradaya">sampradaya</a></i> from father to son or from teacher (<i>guru</i>) to student (<i>shishya</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1995344_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1995344-76">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroo201692_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroo201692-78">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHexam2011chapter_8_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHexam2011chapter_8-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPruthi2004286_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPruthi2004286-79">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood2007_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2007-23">[22]</a></sup> believed to be initiated by the Vedic <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rishis" title="Rishis">rishis</a></i> who heard the <a href="/wiki/N%C4%81da_yoga" title="NÄda yoga">primordial sounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege2012165_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege2012165-80">[73]</a></sup> Only this tradition, embodied by a living teacher, can teach the correct pronunciation of the sounds and explain hidden meanings, in a way the "dead and entombed manuscript" cannot do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroo201692_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroo201692-78">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[note 8]</a></sup> As Leela Prasad states, "According to <a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Shankara</a>, the "correct tradition" (<i>sampradaya</i>) has as much authority as the written Shastra," explaining that the tradition "bears the authority to clarify and provide direction in the application of knowledge."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrasad2007125_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrasad2007125-82">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emphasis in this transmission<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[note 9]</a></sup> is on the "proper articulation and pronunciation of the Vedic sounds," as prescribed in the <a href="/wiki/Shiksha" title="Shiksha">Shiksha</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011345_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011345-85">[76]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Vedanga" title="Vedanga">Vedanga</a> (Vedic study) of sound as uttered in a Vedic recitation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanerji1989323â324_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanerji1989323â324-86">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011477â495_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011477â495-87">[78]</a></sup> mastering the texts "literally forward and backward in fully acoustic fashion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996345_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996345-77">[70]</a></sup> Houben and Rath note that the Vedic textual tradition cannot simply be characterized as oral, "since it also depends significantly on a memory culture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERath201222_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERath201222-88">[79]</a></sup> The Vedas were preserved with precision with the help of elaborate <a href="/wiki/Vedic_chant" title="Vedic chant">mnemonic techniques</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood2007_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2007-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHexam2011chapter_8_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHexam2011chapter_8-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013-25">[24]</a></sup> such as memorizing the texts in eleven different modes of recitation (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patha" title="Patha">pathas</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996345_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996345-77">[70]</a></sup> using the alphabet as a <a href="/wiki/Art_of_memory" title="Art of memory">mnemotechnical device</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1999122_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1999122-89">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERath201219_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERath201219-90">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[note 10]</a></sup> "matching physical movements (such as nodding the head) with particular sounds and chanting in a group"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoniger2010106_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoniger2010106-92">[82]</a></sup> and visualizing sounds by using <i><a href="/wiki/Mudra" title="Mudra">mudras</a></i> (hand signs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011479_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011479-93">[83]</a></sup> This provided an additional visual confirmation, and also an alternate means to check the reading integrity by the audience, in addition to the audible means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011479_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011479-93">[83]</a></sup> Houben and Rath note that a strong "memory culture" existed in ancient India when texts were transmitted orally, before the advent of writing in the early first millennium CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERath201219_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERath201219-90">[81]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Frits_Staal" title="Frits Staal">Staal</a>, criticising the <a href="/wiki/Jack_Goody" title="Jack Goody">Goody</a>-Watt hypothesis "according to which literacy is more reliable than orality,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchiffman2012171_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchiffman2012171-94">[84]</a></sup> this tradition of oral transmission "is closely related to Indian forms of science," and "by far the more remarkable" than the relatively recent tradition of written transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Staal_discoveries_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Staal_discoveries-97">[note 11]</a></sup>
</p><p>While according to Mookerji <a href="/wiki/Nirukta" title="Nirukta">understanding the meaning</a> (<i>vedarthajnana</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalewicz2004328_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalewicz2004328-98">[87]</a></sup> or <i><a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">artha</a>-<a href="/wiki/Buddhi" title="Buddhi">bodha</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201135_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201135-99">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artha_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-artha-100">[note 12]</a></sup>) of the words of the Vedas was part of the <a href="/wiki/Vedanga" title="Vedanga">Vedic learning</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201135_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201135-99">[88]</a></sup> Holdrege and other Indologists<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346-101">[89]</a></sup> have noted that in the transmission of the <i>Samhitas</i> the emphasis is on the phonology of the sounds (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%9Aabda" title="Åabda">Åabda</a></i>) and not on the meaning (<i><a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">artha</a></i>) of the mantras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346-101">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier200755_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier200755-102">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroo201692_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroo201692-78">[71]</a></sup> Already at the end of the Vedic period their original meaning had become obscure for "ordinary people,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier200755_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier200755-102">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kautas_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kautas-104">[note 13]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nirukta" title="Nirukta">niruktas</a></i>, etymological compendia, were developed to preserve and clarify the original meaning of many Sanskrit words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier200755_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier200755-102">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2016"Sayana,_Vidyaranyaâs_brother"_105-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2016"Sayana,_Vidyaranyaâs_brother"-105'>[92]</a></sup> According to Staal, as referenced by Holdrege, though the mantras may have a discursive meaning, when the mantras are recited in the Vedic rituals "they are disengaged from their original context and are employed in ways that have little or nothing to do with their meaning."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346-101">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[note 14]</a></sup> The words of the mantras are "themselves sacred,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346â347_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346â347-107">[93]</a></sup> and "do not constitute <a href="/wiki/Speech_act" title="Speech act">linguistic utterances</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996347_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996347-26">[25]</a></sup> Instead, as Klostermaier notes, in their application in Vedic rituals they become <a href="/wiki/Magic_(supernatural)" title="Magic (supernatural)">magical</a> sounds, "means to an end."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[note 15]</a></sup> Holdrege notes that there are scarce commentaries on the meaning of the mantras, in contrast to the number of commentaries on the Brahmanas and Upanishads, but states that the lack of emphasis on the "discursive meaning does not necessarily imply that they are meaningless."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346,_347_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346,_347-109">[94]</a></sup> In the Brahmanical perspective, the sounds have their own meaning, mantras are considered as "primordial rhythms of creation", preceding the forms to which they refer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996347_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996347-26">[25]</a></sup> By reciting them the cosmos is regenerated, "by enlivening and nourishing the forms of creation at their base. As long as the purity of the sounds is preserved, the recitation of the <i>mantras</i> will be efficacious, irrespective of whether their discursive meaning is understood by human beings."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996347_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996347-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coward2008_mimamsa_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coward2008_mimamsa-110">[note 16]</a></sup> Frazier further notes that "later Vedic texts sought deeper understanding of the reasons the rituals worked," which indicates that the Brahmin communities considered study to be a "process of understanding."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrazier201134_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrazier201134-111">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>A literary tradition is traceable in post-Vedic times, after the rise of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya period</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[note 17]</a></sup> perhaps earliest in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanvas" title="Kanvas">Kanva</a> recension of the Yajurveda about the 1st century BCE; however oral tradition of transmission remained active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witzel2003_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Witzel2003-75">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Goody" title="Jack Goody">Jack Goody</a> has argued for an earlier literary tradition, concluding that the Vedas bear hallmarks of a literate culture along with oral transmission,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011192_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011192-114">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoody1987_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoody1987-115">[98]</a></sup> but Goody's views have been strongly criticised by Falk, Lopez Jr,. and Staal, though they have also found some support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELopez201635â36_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELopez201635â36-116">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlsonCole201315_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlsonCole201315-117">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Vedas were written down only after 500 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-118">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witzel2003_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-Witzel2003-75">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood2007_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2007-23">[22]</a></sup> but only the orally transmitted texts are regarded as authoritative, given the emphasis on the exact pronunciation of the sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996345_77-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996345-77">[70]</a></sup> Witzel suggests that attempts to write down the Vedic texts towards the end of 1st millennium BCE were unsuccessful, resulting in <i>smriti</i> rules explicitly forbidding the writing down of the Vedas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witzel2003_75-3"><a href="#cite_note-Witzel2003-75">[68]</a></sup> Due to the ephemeral nature of the manuscript material (birch bark or palm leaves), surviving manuscripts rarely surpass an age of a few hundred years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[102]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sampurnanand_Sanskrit_University" title="Sampurnanand Sanskrit University">Sampurnanand Sanskrit University</a> has a Rigveda manuscript from the 14th century;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[103]</a></sup> however, there are a number of older Veda manuscripts in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> that are dated from the 11th century onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vedic_learning">Vedic learning</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sv%C4%81dhy%C4%81ya" title="SvÄdhyÄya">SvÄdhyÄya</a></div>
<p>The Vedas, Vedic rituals and its ancillary sciences called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vedangas" title="Vedangas">Vedangas</a>, were part of the curriculum at ancient universities such as at <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">Nalanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vikramashila" title="Vikramashila">Vikramashila</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuswellLopez2013_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuswellLopez2013-122">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrazier2011[httpsarchiveorgdetailscontinuumcompani00frazpagen48_34]_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrazier2011[httpsarchiveorgdetailscontinuumcompani00frazpagen48_34]-123">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[108]</a></sup> According to Deshpande, "the tradition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_grammarians" title="Sanskrit grammarians">Sanskrit grammarians</a> also contributed significantly to the preservation and interpretation of Vedic texts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeshpande199033_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeshpande199033-126">[109]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Y%C4%81ska" title="YÄska">YÄska</a> (4th c. BCE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMisra200049_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMisra200049-127">[110]</a></sup>) wrote the <i><a href="/wiki/Nirukta" title="Nirukta">Nirukta</a></i>, which reflects the concerns about the loss of meaning of the mantras,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kautas_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kautas-104">[note 13]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="PÄá¹ini">PÄá¹inis</a> (4th c. BCE) <i><a href="/wiki/A%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%AD%C4%81dhy%C4%81y%C4%AB" title="Aá¹£á¹­ÄdhyÄyÄ«">Aá¹£á¹­ÄdhyÄyÄ«</a></i> is the most important surviving text of the VyÄkaraá¹a traditions. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mimamsa" title="Mimamsa">Mimamsa</a> scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sayana" title="Sayana">Sayanas</a> (14th c. CE) major <i>Vedartha Prakasha</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[note 18]</a></sup> is a rare<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996354_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996354-129">[111]</a></sup> commentary on the Vedas, which is also referred to by contemporary scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2016ch.3_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2016ch.3-130">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yaska and Sayana, reflecting an ancient understanding, state that the Veda can be interpreted in three ways, giving "the truth about <a href="/wiki/Rigvedic_deities" title="Rigvedic deities">gods</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dharma#History" title="Dharma">dharma</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parabrahman" title="Parabrahman">parabrahman</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECowardRajaPotter1990106_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECowardRajaPotter1990106-131">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201134_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201134-132">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[note 19]</a></sup> The <i>pÅ«rva-kÄÅda</i> (or <i>karma-kanda</i>), the part of the Veda dealing with ritual, gives knowledge of <i>dharma</i>, "which brings us satisfaction." The <i>uttara-kanda</i> (or <i><a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">jnana-kanda</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[note 20]</a></sup> the part of the Veda dealing with the knowledge of the absolute, gives knowledge of <i>Parabrahma</i>, "which fulfills all of our desires."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201130_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201130-135">[115]</a></sup> According to Holdrege, for the exponents of <i>karma-kandha</i> the Veda is to be "inscribed in the minds and hearts of men" by memorization and recitation, while for the exponents of the <i>jnana-kanda</i> and meditation the Vedas express a transcendental reality which can be approached with mystical means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996355,_356â357_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996355,_356â357-136">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Holdrege notes that in Vedic learning "priority has been given to recitation over interpretation" of the Samhitas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996354_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996354-129">[111]</a></sup> Galewicz states that Sayana, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mimamsa" title="Mimamsa">Mimamsa</a> scholar,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalewicz200440_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalewicz200440-137">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalewicz2011338_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalewicz2011338-138">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2009"237_Sayana"_139-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2009"237_Sayana"-139'>[119]</a></sup> "thinks of the Veda as something to be trained and mastered to be put into practical ritual use," noticing that "it is not the meaning of the mantras that is most essential [...] but rather the perfect mastering of their sound form."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalewicz200441_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalewicz200441-140">[120]</a></sup> According to Galewicz, Sayana saw the purpose (<i>artha</i>) of the Veda as the "<i><a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">artha</a></i> of carrying out sacrifice," giving precedence to the <i>Yajurveda</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalewicz200440_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalewicz200440-137">[117]</a></sup> For Sayana, whether the mantras had meaning depended on the context of their practical usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalewicz200441_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalewicz200441-140">[120]</a></sup> This conception of the Veda, as a repertoire to be mastered and performed, takes precedence over the internal meaning or "autonomous message of the hymns."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalewicz200441â42_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalewicz200441â42-141">[121]</a></sup> Most <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Arauta#Contemporary_practices" title="Årauta">Årauta rituals</a> are not performed in the modern era, and those that are, are rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels2016237â238_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels2016237â238-142">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Radha_Kumud_Mukherjee" title="Radha Kumud Mukherjee">Mukherjee</a> notes that the Rigveda, and Sayana's commentary, contain passages criticizing as fruitless mere recitation of the <i>Åik</i> (words) without understanding their inner meaning or essence, the knowledge of <i>dharma</i> and <i>Parabrahman</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201129â31_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201129â31-143">[123]</a></sup> Mukherjee concludes that in the Rigvedic education of the mantras "the contemplation and comprehension of <a href="/wiki/Nirukta" title="Nirukta">their meaning</a> was considered as more important and vital to education than their mere mechanical repetition and correct pronunciation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201129,_34_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201129,_34-144">[124]</a></sup> Mookei refers to Sayana as stating that "the mastery of texts, <i>akshara-praptÄ«</i>, is followed by <i><a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">artha</a>-<a href="/wiki/Buddhi" title="Buddhi">bodha</a></i>, perception of their meaning."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201135_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201135-99">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artha_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-artha-100">[note 12]</a></sup> Mukherjee explains that the Vedic knowledge was first perceived by the <i>rishis</i> and <i>munis</i>. Only the perfect language of the Vedas, as in contrast to ordinary speech, can reveal these truths, which were preserved by committing them to memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201134â35_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201134â35-146">[126]</a></sup> According to Mukherjee, while these truths are imparted to the student by the memorized texts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201135â36_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201135â36-147">[127]</a></sup> "the realization of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rta" title="Rta">Truth</a>" and the knowledge of <i><a href="/wiki/Paramatman" title="Paramatman">paramatman</a></i> as revealed to the <i>rishis</i> is the real aim of Vedic learning, and not the mere recitation of texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201136_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201136-148">[128]</a></sup> The supreme knowledge of the Absolute, <i><a href="/wiki/Para_Brahman" title="Para Brahman">para Brahman</a>-<a href="/wiki/Jnana_yoga" title="Jnana yoga">jnana</a></i>, the knowledge of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rta" title="Rta">rta</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Satya" title="Satya">satya</a></i>, can be obtained by taking vows of silence and obedience<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji2011196_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji2011196-149">[129]</a></sup> sense-restraint, <i><a href="/wiki/Dhyana_in_Hinduism" title="Dhyana in Hinduism">dhyana</a></i>, the practice of <i><a href="/wiki/Tapas_(Indian_religions)" title="Tapas (Indian religions)">tapas</a></i> (austerities),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201134_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201134-132">[114]</a></sup> and discussing the <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji2011196_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji2011196-149">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[note 21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vedic_schools_or_recensions">Vedic schools or recensions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shakha" title="Shakha">Shakha</a></div>
<p>The four Vedas were transmitted in various <a href="/wiki/Shakha" title="Shakha"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">ÅÄkhÄ</i></span>s</a> (branches, schools).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood_1996_39_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flood_1996_39-152">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witzelthreefour_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-witzelthreefour-153">[132]</a></sup> Each school likely represented an ancient community of a particular area, or kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witzelthreefour_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-witzelthreefour-153">[132]</a></sup> Each school followed its own canon. Multiple recensions are known for each of the Vedas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood_1996_39_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flood_1996_39-152">[131]</a></sup> Thus, states Witzel as well as Renou, in the 2nd millennium BCE, there was likely no canon of one broadly accepted Vedic texts, no Vedic âScriptureâ, but only a canon of various texts accepted by each school. Some of these texts have survived, most lost or yet to be found. Rigveda that survives in modern times, for example, is in only one extremely well preserved school of ÅÃ¥kalya, from a region called <a href="/wiki/Videha" title="Videha">Videha</a>, in modern north <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, south of <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jamisonwitzelvedic_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-jamisonwitzelvedic-154">[133]</a></sup> The Vedic canon in its entirety consists of texts from all the various Vedic schools taken together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witzelthreefour_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-witzelthreefour-153">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Each of the four Vedas were shared by the numerous schools, but revised, interpolated and adapted locally, in and after the Vedic period, giving rise to various recensions of the text. Some texts were revised into the modern era, raising significant debate on parts of the text which are believed to have been corrupted at a later date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[135]</a></sup> The Vedas each have an Index or <a href="/wiki/Anukrama%E1%B9%87%C4%AB" title="Anukramaá¹Ä«">Anukramani</a>, the principal work of this kind being the general Index or <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">SarvÄnukramaá¹Ä«</i></span></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prodigious energy was expended by ancient Indian culture in ensuring that these texts were transmitted from generation to generation with inordinate fidelity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-159">[138]</a></sup> For example, memorization of the sacred <i>Vedas</i> included up to eleven <a href="/wiki/Vedic_chant" title="Vedic chant">forms of recitation</a> of the same text.  The texts were subsequently "proof-read" by comparing the different recited versions.  Forms of recitation included the <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">jaá¹­Ä-pÄá¹­ha</i></span></i> (literally "mesh recitation") in which every two adjacent words in the text were first recited in their original order, then repeated in the reverse order, and finally repeated in the original order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filliozat-p139_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-filliozat-p139-160">[139]</a></sup> That these methods have been effective, is attested to by the preservation of the most ancient Indian religious text, the <i><a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a></i>, as <a href="/wiki/Redaction" title="Redaction">redacted</a> into a single text during the <i>Brahmana</i> period, without any variant readings within that school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filliozat-p139_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-filliozat-p139-160">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Vedas were orally transmitted by memorization for many generations and was written down for the first time around 1200 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[141]</a></sup> However, all printed editions of the Vedas that survive in the modern times are likely the version existing in about the 16th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Four_Vedas">Four Vedas</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar sidebar-collapse nomobile nowraplinks"><tbody><tr><td class="sidebar-pretitle" style="background:#FFC569;padding-top:0.5em;">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Hindu_texts" title="Category:Hindu texts">a series</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_scriptures" title="Hindu scriptures">Hindu scriptures</a></td></tr><tr><th class="sidebar-title-with-pretitle" style="background:#FFC569;padding-top:0.1em;"><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Vedas</a> and their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakhas" title="Shakhas">Shakhas</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-image"><a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Om"><img alt="Om" data-file-height="367" data-file-width="356" decoding="async" height="82" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Om2.svg/80px-Om2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Om2.svg/120px-Om2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Om2.svg/160px-Om2.svg.png 2x" width="80"/></a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content" style="padding-bottom:1.0em;">
