<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent; border-bottom:1px solid #aaa; text-align: center; padding-left:0.4em;"><a href="/wiki/Yajurveda#Shukla_Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Shukla Yajurveda</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content">Kanava<br/>Madhyandin</div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content" style="padding-bottom:1.0em;">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent; border-bottom:1px solid #aaa; text-align: center; padding-left:0.4em;"><a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><a href="/wiki/Shaunaka" title="Shaunaka">Shaunaka</a><br/>Paippalada<br/>Stauda<br/>Mauda<br/>Jajala<br/>Jalada<br/>Kuntap<br/>Brahmavada<br/>Devadarsa<br/>Caranavaidya</div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below" style="text-align:center;">
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047488">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Hinduism" title="Portal:Hinduism">Hinduism portal</a></span></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Vedas_and_Shakhas" title="Template:Vedas and Shakhas"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Vedas_and_Shakhas" title="Template talk:Vedas and Shakhas"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Vedas_and_Shakhas&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The canonical division of the Vedas is fourfold (<i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">turÄ«ya</i></span></i>) viz.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>  (RV)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajurveda</a>  (YV, with the main division <a href="/wiki/Taittiriya_Shakha" title="Taittiriya Shakha">TS</a> vs. <a href="/wiki/Shakha" title="Shakha">VS</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Samaveda" title="Samaveda">Samaveda</a>  (SV)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a>  (AV)</li></ol>
<p>Of these, the first three were the principal original division, also called "<i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">trayÄ« vidyÄ</i></span></i>"; that is, "the triple science" of reciting hymns (Rigveda), performing sacrifices (Yajurveda), and chanting songs (Samaveda).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonell200429â39_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonell200429â39-166">[145]</a></sup> The Rig Veda most likely was composed between <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500 BC and 1200 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dating_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-dating-2">[note 1]</a></sup> Witzel notes that it is the Vedic period itself, where incipient lists divide the Vedic texts into three (trayÄ«) or four branches: Rig, Yajur, Sama and Atharva.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witzelthreefour_153-3"><a href="#cite_note-witzelthreefour-153">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Each Veda has been subclassified into four major text types â the <a href="/wiki/Samhita" title="Samhita">Samhitas</a> (mantras and benedictions), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aranyakas" title="Aranyakas">Aranyakas</a> (text on rituals, ceremonies such as newborn baby's rites of passage, coming of age, marriages, retirement and cremation, sacrifices and symbolic sacrifices), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmanas" title="Brahmanas">Brahmanas</a> (commentaries on rituals, ceremonies and sacrifices), and the <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> (text discussing meditation, philosophy and spiritual knowledge).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gflood_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-gflood-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Bhattacharya_2006_pp._8â14_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-A_Bhattacharya_2006_pp._8â14-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jan_Gonda_1975_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jan_Gonda_1975-12">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Upasana" title="Upasana">Upasanas</a> (short ritual worship-related sections) are considered by some scholars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya20068â14_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya20068â14-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1995351â357_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1995351â357-14">[13]</a></sup> as the fifth part. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Witzel" title="Michael Witzel">Witzel</a> notes that the rituals, rites and ceremonies described in these ancient texts reconstruct to a large degree the Indo-European marriage rituals observed in a region spanning the Indian subcontinent, Persia and the European area, and some greater details are found in the Vedic era texts such as the Grhya SÅ«tras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Only one version of the Rigveda is known to have survived into the modern era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jamisonwitzelvedic_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-jamisonwitzelvedic-154">[133]</a></sup> Several different versions of the Sama Veda and the Atharva Veda are known, and many different versions of the Yajur Veda have been found in different parts of South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>The texts of the Upanishads discuss ideas akin to the heterodox <i>sramana</i>-traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199682_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199682-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rigveda">Rigveda</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:26em; ; background-color: #FFE0BB;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p><a href="/wiki/Nasadiya_Sukta" title="Nasadiya Sukta">Nasadiya Sukta</a> (Hymn of non-Eternity):
</p><p>Who really knows?<br/>
Who can here proclaim it?<br/>
Whence, whence this creation sprang?<br/>
Gods came later, after the creation of this universe.
</p><p>Who then knows whence it has arisen?<br/>
Whether God's will created it, or whether He was mute;<br/>
Only He who is its overseer in highest heaven knows,<br/>
He only knows, or perhaps He does not know.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">â<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rig_Veda" title="Rig Veda">Rig Veda</a> 10.129.6â7<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasadiyasukta_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasadiyasukta-169">[148]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda Samhita</a> is the oldest extant <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indic</a> text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[149]</a></sup>  It is a collection of 1,028 <a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">Vedic Sanskrit</a> <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymns</a> and 10,600 verses in all, organized into ten books (Sanskrit: <i>mandalas</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[150]</a></sup> The hymns are dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Rigvedic_deities" title="Rigvedic deities">Rigvedic deities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>The books were composed by poets from different priestly groups over a period of several centuries between <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500 and 1200Â BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dating_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-dating-2">[note 1]</a></sup> (the early <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic period</a>) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sapta_Sindhu" title="Sapta Sindhu">Sapta Sindhu</a>) region of the northwest <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. According to Michael Witzel, the initial codification of the <i>Rigveda</i> took place at the end of the Rigvedic period at ca. 1200 BCE, in the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuru_(India)" title="Kuru (India)">Kuru</a> kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel1997261_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel1997261-173">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Rigveda is structured based on clear principles. The Veda begins with a small book addressed to Agni, Indra, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soma_(deity)" title="Soma (deity)">Soma</a> and other gods, all arranged according to decreasing total number of hymns in each deity collection; for each deity series, the hymns progress from longer to shorter ones, but the number of hymns per book increases. Finally, the meter too is systematically arranged from jagati and tristubh to anustubh and gayatri as the text progresses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witzelthreefour_153-4"><a href="#cite_note-witzelthreefour-153">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rituals became increasingly complex over time, and the king's association with them strengthened both the position of the Brahmans and the kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrasad2020150â151_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrasad2020150â151-174">[153]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rajasuya" title="Rajasuya">Rajasuya</a> rituals, performed with the coronation of a king, "set in motion [...] cyclical regenerations of the universe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrasad2020151_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrasad2020151-175">[154]</a></sup> In terms of substance, the nature of hymns shift from praise of deities in early books to <a href="/wiki/Nasadiya_Sukta" title="Nasadiya Sukta">Nasadiya Sukta</a> with questions such as, "what is the origin of the universe?, do even gods know the answer?",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasadiyasukta_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasadiyasukta-169">[148]</a></sup> the virtue of <a href="/wiki/D%C4%81na" title="DÄna">DÄna</a> (charity) in society,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[155]</a></sup> and other metaphysical issues in its hymns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[note 22]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are similarities between the mythology, rituals and linguistics in Rigveda and those found in ancient central Asia, Iranian and Hindukush (Afghanistan) regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Samaveda">Samaveda</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Samaveda" title="Samaveda">Samaveda</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Samaveda" title="Samaveda">Samaveda Samhita</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[157]</a></sup> consists of 1549 stanzas, taken almost entirely (except for 75 mantras) from the Rigveda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michaels_2004_51_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Michaels_2004_51-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witzelsamaveda_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-witzelsamaveda-180">[158]</a></sup> While its earliest parts are believed to date from as early as the Rigvedic period, the existing compilation dates from the post-Rigvedic Mantra period of <a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">Vedic Sanskrit</a>, between c. 1200 and 1000 BCE or "slightly later," roughly contemporary with the <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajurveda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witzelsamaveda_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-witzelsamaveda-180">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Samaveda samhita has two major parts. The first part includes four melody collections (gÄna, à¤à¤¾à¤¨) and the second part three verse âbooksâ (Ärcika, à¤à¤°à¥à¤à¤¿à¤).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witzelsamaveda_180-2"><a href="#cite_note-witzelsamaveda-180">[158]</a></sup> A melody in the song books corresponds to a verse in the <i>arcika</i> books. Just as in the Rigveda, the early sections of Samaveda typically begin with hymns to Agni and Indra but shift to the abstract. Their meters shift also in a descending order. The songs in the later sections of the Samaveda have the least deviation from the hymns derived from the Rigveda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witzelsamaveda_180-3"><a href="#cite_note-witzelsamaveda-180">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Samaveda, some of the Rigvedic verses are repeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[159]</a></sup> Including repetitions, there are a total of 1875 verses numbered in the Samaveda recension translated by Griffith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[160]</a></sup> Two major recensions have survived, the Kauthuma/Ranayaniya and the Jaiminiya. Its purpose was liturgical, and they were the repertoire of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Udgatr" title="Udgatr"><i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">udgÄtá¹</i></span></i></a> or "singer" priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011381_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011381-183">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yajurveda">Yajurveda</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajurveda</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajurveda Samhita</a> consists of prose mantras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel200376â77_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel200376â77-184">[162]</a></sup> It is a compilation of ritual offering formulas that were said by a priest while an individual performed ritual actions such as those before the <a href="/wiki/Yajna" title="Yajna">yajna</a> fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel200376â77_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel200376â77-184">[162]</a></sup> The core text of the Yajurveda falls within the classical Mantra period of <a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">Vedic Sanskrit</a> at the end of the 2nd millennium BCE - younger than the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>, and roughly contemporary with the <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a>, the Rigvedic <a href="/wiki/Khilani" title="Khilani">Khilani</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Samaveda" title="Samaveda"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">SÄmaveda</i></span></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[163]</a></sup> Witzel dates the Yajurveda <a href="/wiki/Music_of_India#History" title="Music of India">hymns</a> to the early Indian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_Age_India" title="Iron Age India">Iron Age</a>, after c. 1200 and before 800 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[164]</a></sup> corresponding to the early <a href="/wiki/Kuru_Kingdom" title="Kuru Kingdom">Kuru Kingdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taittiriya_Samhita_Vedas,_Devanagari_script,_Sanskrit_pliv.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1696" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Taittiriya_Samhita_Vedas%2C_Devanagari_script%2C_Sanskrit_pliv.jpg/260px-Taittiriya_Samhita_Vedas%2C_Devanagari_script%2C_Sanskrit_pliv.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Taittiriya_Samhita_Vedas%2C_Devanagari_script%2C_Sanskrit_pliv.jpg/390px-Taittiriya_Samhita_Vedas%2C_Devanagari_script%2C_Sanskrit_pliv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Taittiriya_Samhita_Vedas%2C_Devanagari_script%2C_Sanskrit_pliv.jpg/520px-Taittiriya_Samhita_Vedas%2C_Devanagari_script%2C_Sanskrit_pliv.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taittiriya_Samhita_Vedas,_Devanagari_script,_Sanskrit_pliv.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the <i>Taittiriya Samhita</i>, a layer of text within the <i>Yajurveda</i></div></div></div>
<p>The earliest and most ancient layer of Yajurveda <a href="/wiki/Samhita" title="Samhita">samhita</a> includes about 1,875 verses, that are distinct yet borrow and build upon the foundation of verses in <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[166]</a></sup> Unlike the Samaveda which is almost entirely based on Rigveda mantras and structured as songs, the Yajurveda samhitas are in prose, and they are different from earlier Vedic texts linguistically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[167]</a></sup> The Yajur Veda has been the primary source of information about sacrifices during Vedic times and associated rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two major groups of texts in this Veda: the "Black" (<i>Krishna</i>) and the "White" (<i>Shukla</i>). The term "black" implies "the un-arranged, motley collection" of verses in Yajurveda, in contrast to the "white" (well arranged) Yajurveda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pauldeussen217_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-pauldeussen217-191">[169]</a></sup> The White Yajurveda separates the Samhita from its Brahmana (the <a href="/wiki/Shatapatha_Brahmana" title="Shatapatha Brahmana">Shatapatha Brahmana</a>), the Black Yajurveda intersperses the Samhita with Brahmana commentary. Of the Black Yajurveda, texts from four major schools have survived (Maitrayani, Katha, Kapisthala-Katha, Taittiriya), while of the White Yajurveda, two (Kanva and Madhyandina).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200452_Table_3_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200452_Table_3-192">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prabhakar_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-prabhakar-193">[171]</a></sup> The youngest layer of Yajurveda text is not related to rituals nor sacrifice, it includes the largest collection of primary Upanishads, influential to various schools of <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Philosophy_of_the_Upanishads_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Philosophy_of_the_Upanishads-194">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick_Olivelle_1998_pp._1â17_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick_Olivelle_1998_pp._1â17-195">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Artharvaveda Samhita</a> is the text 'belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Atharvan" title="Atharvan">Atharvan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiras_(sage)" title="Angiras (sage)">Angirasa</a> poets. It has about 760 hymns, and about 160 of the hymns are in common with the Rigveda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200456_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200456-196">[174]</a></sup> Most of the verses are metrical, but some sections are in prose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200456_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200456-196">[174]</a></sup> Two different versions of the text â the <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">PaippalÄda</i></span> and the <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">ÅaunakÄ«ya</i></span> â have survived into the modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200456_196-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200456-196">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fritsstaal136_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-fritsstaal136-197">[175]</a></sup> The Atharvaveda was not considered as a Veda in the Vedic era, and was accepted as a Veda in late 1st millennium BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[177]</a></sup> It was compiled last,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zaehnerlastav_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-zaehnerlastav-200">[178]</a></sup> probably around 900 BCE, although some of its material may go back to the time of the Rigveda,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199637_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199637-3">[2]</a></sup> or earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200456_196-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200456-196">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Atharvaveda is sometimes called the "Veda of magical formulas",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lauriepatton38_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-lauriepatton38-201">[179]</a></sup> an epithet declared to be incorrect by other scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jangonda277_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-jangonda277-202">[180]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Samhita" title="Samhita">Samhita</a> layer of the text likely represents a developing 2nd millennium BCE tradition of magico-religious rites to address superstitious anxiety, spells to remove maladies believed to be caused by demons, and herbs- and nature-derived potions as medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[182]</a></sup> The text, states Kenneth Zysk, is one of oldest surviving record of the evolutionary practices in religious medicine and reveals the "earliest forms of folk healing of Indo-European antiquity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[183]</a></sup> Many books of the Atharvaveda Samhita are dedicated to rituals without magic, such as to philosophical speculations and to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theosophy_(Blavatskian)" title="Theosophy (Blavatskian)">theosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jangonda277_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-jangonda277-202">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Atharva veda has been a primary source for information about Vedic culture, the customs and beliefs, the aspirations and frustrations of everyday Vedic life, as well as those associated with kings and governance. The text also includes hymns dealing with the two major rituals of passage â <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cremation" title="Cremation">cremation</a>. The Atharva Veda also dedicates significant portion of the text asking the meaning of a ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Embedded_Vedic_texts">Embedded Vedic texts</span></h3>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brahmanas">Brahmanas</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmanas" title="Brahmanas">Brahmanas</a></div>
<p>The Brahmanas are commentaries, explanation of proper methods and meaning of Vedic Samhita rituals in the four Vedas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier199467â69_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier199467â69-40">[38]</a></sup> They also incorporate myths, legends and in some cases philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier199467â69_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier199467â69-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebri_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-ebri-41">[39]</a></sup> Each regional Vedic <i><a href="/wiki/Shakha" title="Shakha">shakha</a></i> (school) has its own operating manual-like Brahmana text, most of which have been lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winter_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-winter-207">[185]</a></sup> A total of 19 Brahmana texts have survived into modern times: two associated with the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>, six with the <a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajurveda</a>, ten with the <a href="/wiki/Samaveda" title="Samaveda">Samaveda</a> and one with the <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a>. The oldest dated to about 900 BCE, while the youngest Brahmanas (such as the <a href="/wiki/Shatapatha_Brahmana" title="Shatapatha Brahmana">Shatapatha Brahmana</a>), were complete by about 700 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-mw-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcp_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-bcp-43">[41]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Jan_Gonda" title="Jan Gonda">Jan Gonda</a>, the final codification of the Brahmanas took place in pre-Buddhist times (ca. 600 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier199467_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlostermaier199467-208">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>The substance of the Brahmana text varies with each Veda. For example, the first chapter of the Chandogya Brahmana, one of the oldest Brahmanas, includes eight ritual <i>suktas</i> (hymns) for the ceremony of marriage and rituals at the birth of a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maxmullercb_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-maxmullercb-209">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pauldeussencb_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-pauldeussencb-210">[188]</a></sup> The first hymn is a recitation that accompanies offering a <a href="/wiki/Yajna" title="Yajna">Yajna</a> oblation to <i>Agni</i> (fire) on the occasion of a marriage, and the hymn prays for prosperity of the couple getting married.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maxmullercb_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-maxmullercb-209">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[189]</a></sup> The second hymn wishes for their long life, kind relatives, and a numerous progeny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maxmullercb_209-2"><a href="#cite_note-maxmullercb-209">[187]</a></sup> The third hymn is a mutual marriage pledge, between the bride and groom, by which the two bind themselves to each other. The sixth through last hymns of the first chapter in Chandogya Brahmana are ritual celebrations on the birth of a child and wishes for health, wealth, and prosperity with a profusion of cows and <a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">artha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maxmullercb_209-3"><a href="#cite_note-maxmullercb-209">[187]</a></sup> However, these verses are incomplete expositions, and their complete context emerges only with the Samhita layer of text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aranyakas_and_Upanishads">Aranyakas and Upanishads</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aranyakas" title="Aranyakas">Aranyakas</a></div>
<p>The Aranyakas layer of the Vedas include rituals, discussion of symbolic meta-rituals, as well as philosophical speculations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1995351â357_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1995351â357-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jago_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-jago-44">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Aranyakas</i>, however, neither are homogeneous in content nor in structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jago_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-jago-44">[42]</a></sup> They are a medley of instructions and ideas, and some include chapters of Upanishads within them. Two theories have been proposed on the origin of the word <i>Aranyakas</i>. One theory holds that these texts were meant to be studied in a forest, while the other holds that the name came from these being the manuals of allegorical interpretation of sacrifices, for those in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vanaprastha" title="Vanaprastha">Vanaprastha</a> (retired, forest-dwelling) stage of their life, according to the historic age-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashrama_(stage)" title="Ashrama (stage)">Ashrama</a> system of human life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Upanishads reflect the last composed layer of texts in the Vedas. They are commonly referred to as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ved%C4%81nta" title="VedÄnta">VedÄnta</a></i>, variously interpreted to mean either the "last chapters, parts of the Vedas" or "the object, the highest purpose of the Veda".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[192]</a></sup> 
The central concern of the Upanishads are the connections "between parts of the human organism and cosmic realities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1998liii_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1998liii-215">[193]</a></sup> The Upanishads intend to create a hierarchy of connected and dependent realities, evoking a sense of unity of "the separate elements of the world and of human experience [compressing] them into a single form."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1998lv_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1998lv-216">[194]</a></sup> The concepts of <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>, the Ultimate Reality from which everything arises, and <a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ätman (Hinduism)">Ätman</a>, the essence of the individual, are central ideas in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upanishad" title="Upanishad">Upanishads</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMahadevan195259_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMahadevan195259-217">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ptraju_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-ptraju-218">[196]</a></sup> and knowing the correspondence between Ätman and Brahman as "the fundamental principle which shapes the world" permits the creation of an integrative vision of the whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1998lv_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1998lv-216">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ptraju_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-ptraju-218">[196]</a></sup> The Upanishads are the foundation of Hindu philosophical thought and its diverse traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wendydoniger_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-wendydoniger-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[197]</a></sup> and of the Vedic corpus, they alone are widely known, and the central ideas of the Upanishads have influenced the diverse traditions of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wendydoniger_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-wendydoniger-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Aranyakas</i> are sometimes identified as <i>karma-kanda</i> (ritualistic section), while the Upanishads are identified as <i>jnana-kanda</i> (spirituality section).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege199630_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege199630-54">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura1983409_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura1983409-55">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya20069_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya20069-56">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roer_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roer-58">[note 5]</a></sup> In an alternate classification, the early part of Vedas are called <a href="/wiki/Samhita" title="Samhita">Samhitas</a> and the commentary are called the <a href="/wiki/Brahmana" title="Brahmana">Brahmanas</a> which together are identified as the ceremonial <i>karma-kanda</i>, while <i>Aranyakas</i> and <i>Upanishads</i> are referred to as the <i>jnana-kanda</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnapp200510â11_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnapp200510â11-57">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Vedic_literature">Post-Vedic literature</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vedanga">Vedanga</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vedanga" title="Vedanga">Vedanga</a></div>
<p>The Vedangas developed towards the end of the vedic period, around or after the middle of the 1st millennium BCE. These auxiliary fields of Vedic studies emerged because the language of the Vedas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[199]</a></sup> composed centuries earlier, became too archaic to the people of that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1999xxiii_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1999xxiii-222">[200]</a></sup> The Vedangas were sciences that focused on helping understand and interpret the Vedas that had been composed many centuries earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1999xxiii_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1999xxiii-222">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p>The six subjects of Vedanga are phonetics (<a href="/wiki/Shiksha" title="Shiksha"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Åiká¹£Ä</i></span></a>), poetic meter (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_prosody" title="Sanskrit prosody"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Chandas</i></span></a>), grammar (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vyakarana" title="Vyakarana"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">VyÄkaraá¹a</i></span></a>), etymology and linguistics (<a href="/wiki/Nirukta" title="Nirukta">Nirukta</a>), rituals and rites of passage (<a href="/wiki/Kalpa_(Vedanga)" title="Kalpa (Vedanga)"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Kalpa</i></span></a>), time keeping and astronomy (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jyotisha" title="Jyotisha"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Jyotiá¹£a</i></span></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jameslochtefeldvedanga_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-jameslochtefeldvedanga-223">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011391â394_with_footnotes,_416â419_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011391â394_with_footnotes,_416â419-224">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECowardRajaPotter1990105â110_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECowardRajaPotter1990105â110-225">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vedangas developed as ancillary studies for the Vedas, but its insights into meters, structure of sound and language, grammar, linguistic analysis and other subjects influenced post-Vedic studies, arts, culture and various schools of <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011472â532_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011472â532-227">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECowardRajaPotter199018_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECowardRajaPotter199018-228">[206]</a></sup> The Kalpa Vedanga studies, for example, gave rise to the Dharma-sutras, which later expanded into Dharma-shastras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1999xxiii_222-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlivelle1999xxiii-222">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parisista">Parisista</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pari%C5%9Bi%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%ADa" title="PariÅiá¹£á¹­a">PariÅiá¹£á¹­a</a></div>
<p><i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">PariÅiá¹£á¹­a</i></span></i>  "supplement, appendix"  is the term applied to various ancillary works of Vedic literature, dealing mainly with details of ritual and elaborations of the texts logically and chronologically prior to them: the <a href="/wiki/Samhita" title="Samhita">Samhitas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brahmana" title="Brahmana">Brahmanas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aranyaka" title="Aranyaka">Aranyakas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kalpa_(Vedanga)" title="Kalpa (Vedanga)">Sutras</a>.  Naturally classified with the Veda to which each pertains, Parisista works exist for each of the four Vedas.  However, only the literature associated with the <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a> is extensive.
</p>
<ul><li>The <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">ÄÅvalÄyana Gá¹hya PariÅiá¹£á¹­a</i></span></i> is a very late text associated with the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a> canon.</li>
<li>The <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Gobhila Gá¹hya PariÅiá¹£á¹­a</i></span></i> is a short metrical text of two chapters, with 113 and 95 verses respectively.</li>
<li>The <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">KÄtiya PariÅiá¹£á¹­as</i></span></i>, ascribed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Katyayana" title="Katyayana"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">KÄtyÄyana</i></span></a>, consist of 18 works enumerated self-referentially in the fifth of the series (the <a href="/wiki/Shakha" title="Shakha"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Caraá¹avyÅ«ha</i></span></a>) and the <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">KÄtyÄyana Årauta SÅ«tra PariÅiá¹£á¹­a</i></span></i>.</li>
<li>The <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Ká¹á¹£á¹a</i></span> <a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajurveda</a> has 3 parisistas The </i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Äpastamba Hautra PariÅiá¹£á¹­a</i></span><i>, which is also found as the second </i>praÅna<i> of the </i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">SatyasÄá¸ha Årauta SÅ«tra</i></span>', the <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">VÄrÄha Årauta SÅ«tra PariÅiá¹£á¹­a</i></span></i></li>
<li>For the Atharvaveda, there are 79 works, collected as 72 distinctly named parisistas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[208]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upaveda">Upaveda</span></h3>
<p>The term <b>upaveda</b> ("applied knowledge") is used in traditional literature to designate the subjects of certain technical works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonier-Williams1899[httpwwwibiblioorgsripediaebooksmw0200mw_0240html_207]_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonier-Williams1899[httpwwwibiblioorgsripediaebooksmw0200mw_0240html_207]-231">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEApte1965293_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEApte1965293-232">[210]</a></sup> Lists of what subjects are included in this class differ among sources.
The <a href="/wiki/Shaunaka" title="Shaunaka">Charanavyuha</a> mentions four Upavedas:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">Archery</a>  (<a href="/wiki/Indian_martial_arts" title="Indian martial arts">Dhanurveda</a>), associated with the Yajurveda</li>
<li>Architecture (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sthapatyaveda" title="Sthapatyaveda">Sthapatyaveda</a>), associated with the Rigveda.</li>
<li>Music and <a href="/wiki/Indian_classical_dance" title="Indian classical dance">sacred dance</a> (<span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gandharvaveda" title="Gandharvaveda">GÄndharvaveda</a></i></span>), associated with the Samaveda</li>
<li>Medicine (<a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Äyurveda</i></span></a>), associated with  the Atharvaveda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[213]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span id=".22Fifth.22_and_other_Vedas"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Fifth"_and_other_Vedas'>"Fifth" and other Vedas</span></h3>
<p>Some post-Vedic texts, including the <a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Natya_Shastra" title="Natya Shastra">Natyasastra</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[214]</a></sup> and certain <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a>, refer to themselves as the "<a href="/wiki/Fifth_Veda" title="Fifth Veda">fifth Veda</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESullivan1994385_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESullivan1994385-237">[215]</a></sup> The earliest reference to such a "fifth Veda" is found in the <a href="/wiki/Chandogya_Upanishad" title="Chandogya Upanishad">Chandogya Upanishad</a> in hymn 7.1.2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[216]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Let drama and dance (NÄtya, à¤¨à¤¾à¤à¥à¤¯) be the fifth vedic scripture. Combined with an epic story, tending to virtue, wealth, joy and spiritual freedom, it must contain the significance of every scripture, and forward every art. Thus, from all the Vedas, <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a> framed the NÄtya Veda. From the Rig Veda he drew forth the words, from the Sama Veda the melody, from the Yajur Veda gesture, and from the Atharva Veda the sentiment.
</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite>First chapter of <i>NÄtyaÅÄstra</i>, Abhinaya Darpana <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[218]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divya_Prabandha" title="Divya Prabandha">Divya Prabandha</a>", for example Tiruvaymoli, is a term for canonical <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a> texts considered as Vernacular Veda by some South Indian Hindus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Carman_1989_pp._259â261_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-John_Carman_1989_pp._259â261-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasudha_Narayanan_1994_pp._43,_117_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vasudha_Narayanan_1994_pp._43,_117-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other texts such as the <a href="/wiki/Bhagavad_Gita" title="Bhagavad Gita">Bhagavad Gita</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Brahma_Sutras" title="Brahma Sutras">Vedanta Sutras</a> are considered <i>shruti</i> or "Vedic" by some <a href="/wiki/Hindu_denominations" title="Hindu denominations">Hindu denominations</a> but not universally within Hinduism. The <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_movement" title="Bhakti movement">Bhakti movement</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gaudiya_Vaishnavism" title="Gaudiya Vaishnavism">Gaudiya Vaishnavism</a> in particular extended the term <i>veda</i> to include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_Epics" title="Sanskrit Epics">Sanskrit Epics</a> and Vaishnavite devotional texts such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pa%C3%B1caratra" title="PaÃ±caratra">Pancaratra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Puranas">Puranas</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a></div>
<p>The <i>Puranas</i> is a vast genre of encyclopedic Indian literature about a wide range of topics particularly myths, legends and other traditional lore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oliverleaman437_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-oliverleaman437-242">[220]</a></sup> Several of these texts are named after major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> deities such as Vishnu, Shiva and Devi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ludorocher_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-ludorocher-243">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nair_2008_266_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nair_2008_266-244">[222]</a></sup> There are 18 <i>Maha Puranas</i> (Great Puranas) and 18 <i>Upa Puranas</i> (Minor Puranas), with over 400,000 verses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oliverleaman437_242-1"><a href="#cite_note-oliverleaman437-242">[220]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Puranas have been influential in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gregbailey442_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-gregbailey442-246">[224]</a></sup> They are considered <i>Vaidika</i> (congruent with Vedic literature).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[225]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bhagavata_Purana" title="Bhagavata Purana">Bhagavata Purana</a> has been among the most celebrated and popular text in the Puranic genre, and is of <a href="/wiki/Nondualism" title="Nondualism">non-dualistic</a> tenor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richardthompson_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-richardthompson-248">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[227]</a></sup> The Puranic literature wove with the <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_movement" title="Bhakti movement">Bhakti movement</a> in India, and both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dvaita" title="Dvaita">Dvaita</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advaita" title="Advaita">Advaita</a> scholars have commented on the underlying Vedanta themes in the <i>Maha Puranas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[228]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Authority_of_the_Vedas">Authority of the Vedas</span></h2>
<p>The various <a href="/wiki/Hindu_denominations" title="Hindu denominations">Hindu denominations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian philosophies</a> have taken differing positions on the authority of the Vedas. Schools of Indian philosophy which acknowledge the authority of the Vedas are classified as "orthodox" (<a href="/wiki/%C4%80stika_and_n%C4%81stika" title="Ästika and nÄstika">Ästika</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[note 23]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Arama%E1%B9%87a" title="Åramaá¹a">Åramaá¹a</a> traditions, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lokayata" title="Lokayata">Lokayata</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carvaka" title="Carvaka">Carvaka</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ajivika" title="Ajivika">Ajivika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, which did not regard the Vedas as authorities, are referred to as "heterodox" or "non-orthodox" (<a href="/wiki/%C4%80stika_and_n%C4%81stika" title="Ästika and nÄstika">nÄstika</a>) schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199682_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199682-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_astika_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB_astika-28">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Certain traditions which are often seen as being part of Hinduism also rejected the Vedas. For example, authors of the tantric <a href="/wiki/Vaishnava-Sahajiya" title="Vaishnava-Sahajiya">Vaiá¹£á¹ava SahajiyÄ</a> tradition, like Siddha Mukundadeva, rejected the Vedas' authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[230]</a></sup> Likewise, some tantric Shaiva Agamas reject the Vedas. The <i>Anandabhairava-tantra</i> for example, states that "the wise man should not elect as his authority the word of the Vedas, which is full of impurity, produces but scanty and transitory fruits and is limited."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though many religious Hindus implicitly acknowledge the authority of the Vedas, this acknowledgment is often "no more than a declaration that someone considers himself [or herself] a Hindu,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipner201216_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipner201216-255">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[note 24]</a></sup> and "most Indians today pay lip service to the Veda and have no regard for the contents of the text."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[233]</a></sup> Some Hindus challenge the authority of the Vedas, thereby implicitly acknowledging its importance to the history of Hinduism, states Lipner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipner201215â17_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipner201215â17-258">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hindu reform movement such as <a href="/wiki/Arya_Samaj" title="Arya Samaj">Arya Samaj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brahmo_Samaj" title="Brahmo Samaj">Brahmo Samaj</a> accepted the authority of Vedas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[235]</a></sup> while the authority of the Vedas has been rejected by <a href="/wiki/Neo-Vedanta" title="Neo-Vedanta">Hindu modernists</a> like <a href="/wiki/Debendranath_Tagore" title="Debendranath Tagore">Debendranath Tagore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keshub_Chandra_Sen" title="Keshub Chandra Sen">Keshub Chandra Sen</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1994272_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1994272-260">[236]</a></sup> and also by social reformers like <a href="/wiki/B._R._Ambedkar" title="B. R. Ambedkar">B. R. Ambedkar</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENagappa2011283_("It_is_said_that_the_Varna_system_[...]_Sanatan_Hindu")_261-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENagappa2011283_("It_is_said_that_the_Varna_system_[...]_Sanatan_Hindu")-261'>[237]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Indology">Western Indology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_studies" title="Sanskrit studies">Sanskrit studies</a></div>
<p>The study of Sanskrit in the West began in the 17th century. In the early 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a> drew attention to Vedic texts, specifically the Upanishads.
The importance of Vedic Sanskrit for <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_studies" title="Indo-European studies">Indo-European studies</a> was also recognized in the early 19th century.
English translations of the Samhitas were published in the later 19th century, in the <i><a href="/wiki/Sacred_Books_of_the_East" title="Sacred Books of the East">Sacred Books of the East</a></i> series edited by <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max MÃ¼ller">MÃ¼ller</a> between 1879 and 1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[238]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Ralph_T._H._Griffith" title="Ralph T. H. Griffith">Ralph T. H. Griffith</a> also presented English translations of the four Samhitas, published 1889 to 1899.
</p><p>Rigveda manuscripts were selected for inscription in <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Memory_of_the_World_Programme" title="Memory of the World Programme">Memory of the World</a> <a href="/wiki/Memory_of_the_World_Register_%E2%80%93_Asia_and_the_Pacific" title="Memory of the World Register â Asia and the Pacific">Register</a> in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[239]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-dating-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-dating_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dating_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dating_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dating_2-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dating_2-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dating_2-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">It is certain that the hymns of the Rig Veda post-date <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Iranian" title="Proto-Indo-Iranian">Indo-Iranian</a> separation of ca. 2000 BC and probably that of the relevant Mitanni documents of <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1400 BC. The oldest available text is estimated to be from 1200 BC. Philological estimates tend to date the bulk of the text to the second half of the second millennium:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max MÃ¼ller">Max MÃ¼ller</a>: "the hymns of the Rig-Veda are said to date from 1500 B.C."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMÃ¼ller1892_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMÃ¼ller1892-264">[240]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_Indo-European_Culture" title="Encyclopedia of Indo-European Culture">EIEC</a> (s.v. <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian languages</a>, p. 306) gives 1500â1000 BC.</li>
<li>Flood and Witzel both mention <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500â1200 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199637_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199637-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel19954_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel19954-62">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li>Anthony mentions <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500â1300 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007454_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007454-70">[64]</a></sup></li>
<li>Thomas Oberlies (<i>Die Religion des Rgveda</i>, 1998, p. 158) based on 'cumulative evidence' sets a wide range of 1700â1100 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOberlies1998158_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOberlies1998158-71">[65]</a></sup> <a href="#CITEREFOberlies1998">Oberlies 1998</a>, p.Â 155 gives an estimate of 1100 BC for the youngest hymns in book 10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOberlies1998155_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOberlies1998155-265">[241]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFWitzel1995">Witzel 1995</a>, p.Â 4 mentions <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500â1200 BC. According to <a href="#CITEREFWitzel1997">Witzel 1997</a>, p.Â 263, the whole Rig Vedic period may have lasted from c. 1900 BCE to c. 1200 BCE: "the bulk of the RV represents only 5 or 6 generations of kings (and of the contemporary poets)24 of the PÅ«ru and Bharata tribes. It contains little else before and after this âsnapshotâ view of contemporary Rgvedic history, as reported by these contemporary âtape recordings.â On the other hand, the whole Rgvedic period may have lasted even up to 700 years, from the infiltration of the Indo-Aryans into the subcontinent, c. 1900 B.C. (at the utmost, the time of collapse of the Indus civilization), up to c. 1200 B.C., the time of the introduction of iron which is first mentioned in the clearly post-vedic hymns of the Atharvaveda."</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Freschi_2012-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Freschi_2012_27-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Elisa Freschi (2012): "The Vedas are not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deontological_ethics" title="Deontological ethics">deontic</a> authorities in absolute sense and may be disobeyed, but are recognized as a deontological <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemic</a> authority by a Hindu orthodox school."<a href="#CITEREFFreschi2012">Freschi 2012</a>, p.Â 62 This differentiation between epistemic and deontic authority is true for all Indian religions.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a table of all Vedic texts see <a href="#CITEREFWitzel2003">Witzel 2003</a>, pp.Â 100â101.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Vedic Sanskrit corpus is incorporated in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Vedic_Word_Concordance" title="A Vedic Word Concordance">A Vedic Word Concordance</a></i> (<i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Vaidika-PadÄnukrama-Koá¹£a</i></span></i>) prepared from 1930 under Vishva Bandhu, and published in five volumes in 1935â1965. Its scope extends to about 400 texts, including the entire Vedic Sanskrit corpus besides some "sub-Vedic" texts. Volume I: Samhitas, Volume II: Brahmanas and Aranyakas, Volume III: Upanishads, Volume IV: Vedangas; A revised edition, extending to about 1800 pages, was published in 1973â1976.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Roer-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Roer_58-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Roer_58-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Edward Roer (Translator), <i><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=3uwDAAAAMAAJ" rel="nofollow">Shankara's Introduction</a></i> at <a href="/wiki/Google_Books" title="Google Books">Google Books</a> to <i>Brihad Aranyaka Upanishad</i> at pp. 1â5: "The Vedas are divided in two parts, the first is the karma-kanda, the ceremonial part, also (called) purva-kanda, and treats on ceremonies; the second part is the jnana kanda, the part which contains knowledge, also named uttara-kanda or posterior part, and unfolds the knowledge of Brahma or the universal soul."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"As a skilled craftsman makes a car, a singer I, Mighty One! this hymn for thee have fashioned. If thou, O Agni, God, accept it gladly, may we obtain thereby the heavenly Waters". â <i>Rigveda 5.2.11</i>, Translated by Ralph T.H. Griffith<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Gavin_Flood" title="Gavin Flood">Gavin Flood</a> sums up mainstream estimates, according to which the Rigveda was compiled from as early as 1500 BCE over a period of several centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199637_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199637-3">[2]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBroo2016">Broo 2016</a>, p.Â 92 quotes Harold G. Coward and K. Kunjunni Raja.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Of the complete Veda, by <i>pÄÈha-ÅÄlÄ</i> (priestly schools), as distinguished from the transmission in the <i>pÅ«jÄ</i>, the daily services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011344â345_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkeMoebus2011344â345-83">[75]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Several authors refer to the Chinese Buddhist Monk I-Tsing, who visited India in the 7th century to retrieve Buddhist texts and gave examples of mnemonic techniques used in India:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1999122_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1999122-89">[80]</a></sup> "In India there are two traditional ways in which one can attain great intellectual power. Firstly by repeatedly committing to memory the intellect is developed; secondly the alphabet fixes (to) one's ideas. By this way, after a practice of ten days or a month, a student feels his thoughts rise like a fountain, and can commit to memory whatever he has heard once."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERath201219_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERath201219-90">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1999122_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1999122-89">[80]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Staal_discoveries-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Staal_discoveries_97-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Staal: [this tradition of oral transmission is] "by far the more remarkable [than the relatively recent tradition of written transmission], not merely because it is characteristically Indian and unlike anything we find elsewhere, but also because it has led to scientific discoveries that are of enduring interest and from which the contemporary West still has much to learn." <a href="#CITEREFSchiffman2012">Schiffman (2012</a>, p.Â 171), quoting <a href="#CITEREFStaal1986">Staal (1986</a>, p.Â 27)<br/>Staal argued that the ancient Indian grammarians, especially <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="PÄá¹ini">PÄá¹ini</a>, had completely mastered methods of linguistic theory not rediscovered again until the 1950s and the applications of modern mathematical logic to linguistics by <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">Noam Chomsky</a>. (Chomsky himself has said that the first <a href="/wiki/Generative_grammar" title="Generative grammar">generative grammar</a> in the modern sense was Panini's grammar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-front_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-front-95">[85]</a></sup> These early Indian methods allowed the construction of discrete, potentially infinite generative systems. Remarkably, these early linguistic systems were codified orally, though writing was then used to develop them in some way. The formal basis for Panini's methods involved the use of "auxiliary" markers, rediscovered in the 1930s by the logician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emil_Post" title="Emil Post">Emil Post</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[86]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-artha-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-artha_100-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-artha_100-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Artha</i> may also mean "goal, purpose or essence," depending on the context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[125]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Kautas-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Kautas_104-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kautas_104-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKlostermaier2007">Klostermaier 2007</a>, p.Â 55: "Kautas, a teacher mentioned in the <i><a href="/wiki/Nirukta" title="Nirukta">Nirukta</a></i> by <i><a href="/wiki/Y%C4%81ska" title="YÄska">YÄska</a></i> (ca. 500 BCE), a work devoted to an etymology of Vedic words that were no longer understood by ordinary people, held that the word of the Veda was no longer perceived as meaningful "normal" speech but as a fixed sequence of sounds, whose meaning was obscure beyond recovery."<br/><br/>The tenth through twelfth volumes of the first Prapathaka of the <a href="/wiki/Chandogya_Upanishad" title="Chandogya Upanishad">Chandogya Upanishad</a> (800-600 BCE) describe a legend about priests and it criticizes how they go about reciting verses and singing hymns without any idea what they mean or the divine principle they signify.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pauldeussen112_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-pauldeussen112-103">[91]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-106"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-106">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Holdrege, <i>srotriyas</i> (a  group of male Brahmin reciters who are masters of <i>sruti</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996345_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996345-77">[70]</a></sup>) "frequently do not understand what they recite" when reciting the <i>Samhitas</i>, merely preserving the sound of the text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346_101-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoldrege1996346-101">[89]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Klostermaier: "<i>Brahman</i>, derived from the root <i>bÅh</i> = to grow, to become great, was originally identical with the Vedic word, that makes people prosper: words were the pricipan means to approach the gods who dwelled in a different sphere. It was not a big step from this notion of "reified <a href="/wiki/Speech_act" title="Speech act">speech-act</a>" to that "of the speech-act being looked at implicitly and explicitly as a means to an end." <a href="#CITEREFKlostermaier2007">Klostermaier 2007</a>, p.Â 55 quotes <a href="#CITEREFDeshpande1990">Deshpande 1990</a>, p.Â 4.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Coward2008_mimamsa-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Coward2008_mimamsa_110-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCoward2008">Coward 2008</a>, p.Â 114: "For the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mimamsa" title="Mimamsa">Mimamsa</a> the ultimate reality is nothing other than the eternal words of the Vedas. They did not accept the existence of a single supreme creator god, who might have composed the Veda. According to the Mimamsa, gods named in the Vedas have no existence apart from the mantras that speak their names. The power of the gods, then, is nothing other than the power of the mantras that name them."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-113">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The early Buddhist texts are also generally believed to be of oral tradition, with the first Pali Canon written many centuries after the death of the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[96]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Literally, "the meaning of the Vedas made manifest."</span>

<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sayana repeats Yaska; see <a href="/wiki/Nirukta#Ancient" title="Nirukta">interpretation of the Vedas</a>.</span>

<li id="cite_note-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-134">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>Upanishads</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura1983409_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura1983409-55">[51]</a></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-151"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-151">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mookerji also refers to the UÅanÄ smriti (81-2), which "states that mastery of mere text of Veda is to be followed up by its meaning" by discussing the Vedanta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji2011196_149-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji2011196-149">[129]</a></sup> where-after they were able to engage in doscourses on the Vedas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji201129_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji201129-150">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrazier201134_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrazier201134-111">[95]</a></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-177"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-177">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example,<br/><i>Hymn 1.164.34</i>, "What is the ultimate limit of the earth?", "What is the center of the universe?", "What is the semen of the cosmic horse?", "What is the ultimate source of human speech?"<br/><i>Hymn 1.164.34</i>, "Who gave blood, soul, spirit to the earth?", "How could the unstructured universe give origin to this structured world?"<br/><i>Hymn 1.164.5</i>, "Where does the sun hide in the night?", "Where do gods live?"<br/><i>Hymn 1.164.6</i>, "What, where is the unborn support for the born universe?";<br/><i>Hymn 1.164.20</i> (a hymn that is widely cited in the Upanishads as the parable of the Body and the Soul): "Two birds with fair wings, inseparable companions; Have found refuge in the same sheltering tree.
